      <p>
       <b>
        Metro Atlanta
       </b>
       , designated by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Office_of_Management_and_Budget" title="United States Office of Management and Budget">
        United States Office of Management and Budget
       </a>
       as the
       <b>
        Atlanta–Sandy Springs–Roswell, GA Metropolitan Statistical Area
       </b>
       , is the most populous metro area in the US state of
       <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">
        Georgia
       </a>
       and the ninth-largest
       <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_statistical_area" title="Metropolitan statistical area">
        metropolitan statistical area
       </a>
       (MSA) in the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">
        United States
       </a>
       . Its economic, cultural and demographic center is
       <a href="/wiki/Atlanta" title="Atlanta">
        Atlanta
       </a>
       , and it had a 2015 estimated population of 5.7 million people according to the U.S. Census Bureau. The metro area forms the core of a broader trading area, the
       <b>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Combined_Statistical_Area" title="Combined Statistical Area">
         Atlanta–Athens-Clarke–Sandy Springs Combined Statistical Area
        </a>
       </b>
       . The Combined Statistical Area spans up to 39 counties in
       <a href="/wiki/North_Georgia" title="North Georgia">
        north Georgia
       </a>
       and had an estimated 2015 population of 6.3 million people.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Atlanta is considered a "
       <a href="/wiki/Global_city" title="Global city">
        beta(+) world city
       </a>
       ."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is the third largest metropolitan region in the Census Bureau's
       <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_United_States" title="Southeastern United States">
        Southeast
       </a>
       region behind
       <a href="/wiki/Washington_metropolitan_area" title="Washington metropolitan area">
        Greater Washington
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Miami_metropolitan_area" title="Miami metropolitan area">
        South Florida
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions">
        Definitions
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlanta_metropolitan_area&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definitions">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Atlanta-metroclean.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="258" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Atlanta-metroclean.png/180px-Atlanta-metroclean.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ed/Atlanta-metroclean.png 1.5x" width="180"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Atlanta-metroclean.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Location in Georgia (MSA counties in Red).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       By
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Census_Bureau" title="U.S. Census Bureau">
        U.S. Census Bureau
       </a>
       standards, the population of the Atlanta region spreads across a metropolitan area of 8,376 square miles (21,694 km
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       ) – a land area comparable to that of
       <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">
        Massachusetts
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MACOC-growth_4-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-MACOC-growth-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Because Georgia contains more counties than any other state except
       <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">
        Texas
       </a>
       (explained in part by the now-defunct
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/County-unit_system" title="County-unit system">
        county-unit system
       </a>
       of weighing votes in
       <a href="/wiki/Primary_election" title="Primary election">
        primary elections
       </a>
       ),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       area residents live under a heavily decentralized collection of governments. As of the 2000 census, fewer than one in ten residents of the metropolitan area lived inside Atlanta
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/City_limit" title="City limit">
        city limits
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A 2006 survey by the
       <a href="/wiki/Metro_Atlanta_Chamber_of_Commerce" title="Metro Atlanta Chamber of Commerce">
        Metro Atlanta Chamber of Commerce
       </a>
       counted 140 cities and towns in the 28‑county Metropolitan Statistical Area in mid-2005.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MACOC-growth_4-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-MACOC-growth-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Six cities –
       <a href="/wiki/Johns_Creek,_Georgia" title="Johns Creek, Georgia">
        Johns Creek
       </a>
       (2006),
       <a href="/wiki/Milton,_Georgia" title="Milton, Georgia">
        Milton
       </a>
       (2006),
       <a href="/wiki/Chattahoochee_Hills,_Georgia" title="Chattahoochee Hills, Georgia">
        Chattahoochee Hills
       </a>
       (2007),
       <a href="/wiki/Dunwoody,_Georgia" title="Dunwoody, Georgia">
        Dunwoody
       </a>
       (2008),
       <a href="/wiki/Peachtree_Corners,_Georgia" title="Peachtree Corners, Georgia">
        Peachtree Corners
       </a>
       (2012), and
       <a href="/wiki/Brookhaven,_Georgia" title="Brookhaven, Georgia">
        Brookhaven
       </a>
       (2012) – have incorporated since then, following the lead of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sandy_Springs" title="Sandy Springs">
        Sandy Springs
       </a>
       in 2005.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Atlanta metropolitan area was first defined in 1950 as Fulton, DeKalb, Gwinnett, Cobb and Clayton counties. Walton, Newton, Douglas, Fayette, Forsyth, Henry, Cherokee, Rockdale and Butts counties were added after the 1970 census, with Barrow and Coweta counties joining in 1980 and Bartow, Carroll, Paulding, Pickens and Spalding counties in 1990.
      </p>
      <p>
       Atlanta's larger
       <a href="/wiki/Combined_statistical_area" title="Combined statistical area">
        combined statistical area
       </a>
       (CSA) adds the Gainesville, Georgia MSA, Athens-Clarke County, Georgia MSA and the
       <a href="/wiki/LaGrange,_Georgia" title="LaGrange, Georgia">
        LaGrange
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Thomaston,_Georgia" title="Thomaston, Georgia">
        Thomaston
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Jefferson,_Georgia" title="Jefferson, Georgia">
        Jefferson
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Calhoun,_Georgia" title="Calhoun, Georgia">
        Calhoun
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Cedartown,_Georgia" title="Cedartown, Georgia">
        Cedartown
       </a>
       micropolitan areas, for a total 2012 population of 6,162,195. The CSA also abuts the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macon,_Georgia_metropolitan_area" title="Macon, Georgia metropolitan area">
        Macon
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Columbus,_Georgia_metropolitan_area" title="Columbus, Georgia metropolitan area">
        Columbus
       </a>
       MSAs. The region is one of the metropolises of the Southeastern United States, and is part of the emerging
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megalopolis_(city_type)" title="Megalopolis (city type)">
        megalopolis
       </a>
       known as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Piedmont_Atlantic_MegaRegion" title="Piedmont Atlantic MegaRegion">
        Piedmont Atlantic MegaRegion
       </a>
       along the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/I-85_Corridor" title="I-85 Corridor">
        I-85 Corridor
       </a>
       , "Charlanta" colloquially.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>

      <p>
       The above-listed counties are included in the Atlanta–Sandy Springs–Gainesville CSA. (Bold counties are also in the somewhat smaller Atlanta–Sandy Springs–Roswell MSA.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ) However, most other entities define a much smaller metropolitan area by including only the counties which have the densest suburban development. Fulton, DeKalb, Gwinnett, Cobb, and Clayton were the five original counties when the Atlanta metropolitan area was first defined in 1950, and continue to be the core of the metro area. These five counties along with five more (Cherokee, Douglas, Fayette, Henry and Rockdale) are members of the
       <a href="/wiki/Atlanta_Regional_Commission" title="Atlanta Regional Commission">
        Atlanta Regional Commission
       </a>
       , a weak
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metropolitan_government" title="Metropolitan government">
        metropolitan government
       </a>
       agency which also is a
       <a href="/wiki/Regional_planning" title="Regional planning">
        regional planning
       </a>
       agency. The ten ARC counties and five more (Bartow, Coweta, Hall, Forsyth, Paulding) form part of the
       <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_North_Georgia_Water_Planning_District" title="Metropolitan North Georgia Water Planning District">
        Metropolitan North Georgia Water Planning District
       </a>
       , created in 2001.
      </p>
      <p>
       The 14 counties listed above with under 60,000 residents are usually not included in any other metropolitan definition except the OMB/Census Bureau's MSA and CSA. Hall County forms the Gainesville, GA Metropolitan Statistical Area, but with astronomical growth to over 180,000 residents, is now also part of the Atlanta CSA. The official tourism website of the State of Georgia features a
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.exploregeorgia.org/AtlantaMetro" rel="nofollow">
        "Metro Atlanta" tourism region
       </a>
       that includes only nine counties: Fulton, DeKalb, Gwinnett, Cobb, Clayton, Coweta, Douglas, Fayette, and Henry.
      </p>

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Edge_cities">
        Edge cities
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlanta_metropolitan_area&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Edge cities">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cumberland_(Atlanta)" title="Cumberland (Atlanta)">
         Cumberland
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Perimeter_Center" title="Perimeter Center">
         Perimeter Center
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Monroe,_Georgia" title="Monroe, Georgia">
         Monroe, Georgia
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hartsfield-Jackson_Atlanta_International_Airport" title="Hartsfield-Jackson Atlanta International Airport">
         Hartsfield-Jackson
        </a>
        area
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       More than one half of metro Atlanta's population is in unincorporated areas or areas considered a
       <a href="/wiki/Census-designated_place" title="Census-designated place">
        census-designated-place
       </a>
       (CDP) by the census bureau. Metro Atlanta includes the following incorporated and unincorporated suburbs (both inside and outside Atlanta), exurbs, and surrounding cities, sorted by population as of 2010:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">
        Geography
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlanta_metropolitan_area&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Geography">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb noresize tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:707px;">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="">
        <div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Atlanta_Skyline,_May_2013.jpg" title="The topography and geography of Atlanta">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="3581" data-file-width="16183" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Atlanta_Skyline%2C_May_2013.jpg/699px-Atlanta_Skyline%2C_May_2013.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Atlanta_Skyline%2C_May_2013.jpg/1049px-Atlanta_Skyline%2C_May_2013.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Atlanta_Skyline%2C_May_2013.jpg/1398px-Atlanta_Skyline%2C_May_2013.jpg 2x" width="699"/>
         </a>
        </div>
        <div class="thumbcaption" style="text-align:center">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Atlanta_Skyline,_May_2013.jpg" title="File:Atlanta Skyline, May 2013.jpg">
          </a>
         </div>
         The topography and geography of Atlanta
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Topography_and_geology">
        Topography and geology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlanta_metropolitan_area&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Topography and geology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The area sprawls across the low
       <a href="/wiki/Foothills" title="Foothills">
        foothills
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Appalachian_Mountains" title="Appalachian Mountains">
        Appalachian Mountains
       </a>
       to the north and the
       <a href="/wiki/Piedmont_(United_States)" title="Piedmont (United States)">
        Piedmont
       </a>
       to the south. The northern and some western suburbs tend to be higher and significantly more
       <a href="/wiki/Hill" title="Hill">
        hilly
       </a>
       than the southern and eastern suburbs. The average elevation is around 1,000 feet (300 m).
      </p>
      <p>
       The highest point in the immediate area is
       <a href="/wiki/Kennesaw_Mountain" title="Kennesaw Mountain">
        Kennesaw Mountain
       </a>
       at 1,808 ft (551 m), followed by
       <a href="/wiki/Stone_Mountain" title="Stone Mountain">
        Stone Mountain
       </a>
       at 1,686 ft (514 m),
       <a href="/wiki/Sweat_Mountain" title="Sweat Mountain">
        Sweat Mountain
       </a>
       at 1,640 ft (500 m), and
       <a href="/wiki/Little_Kennesaw_Mountain" title="Little Kennesaw Mountain">
        Little Kennesaw Mountain
       </a>
       at 1,600 ft (488 m). Others include
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Blackjack_Mountain_(Cobb_County,_Georgia)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Blackjack Mountain (Cobb County, Georgia) (page does not exist)">
        Blackjack Mountain
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Lost_Mountain" title="Lost Mountain">
        Lost Mountain
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Brushy_Mountain_(Cobb_County,_Georgia)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Brushy Mountain (Cobb County, Georgia) (page does not exist)">
        Brushy Mountain
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Pine_Mountain_(Cobb_County,_Georgia)" title="Pine Mountain (Cobb County, Georgia)">
        Pine Mountain
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Mount_Wilkinson" title="Mount Wilkinson">
        Mount Wilkinson
       </a>
       (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vinings_Mountain" title="Vinings Mountain">
        Vinings Mountain
       </a>
       ). Many of these play prominently in the various
       <a href="/wiki/Battle" title="Battle">
        battles
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Atlanta_Campaign" title="Atlanta Campaign">
        Atlanta Campaign
       </a>
       during the
       <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">
        American Civil War
       </a>
       . If the further-north counties are included,
       <a href="/wiki/Bear_Mountain_(Georgia)" title="Bear Mountain (Georgia)">
        Bear Mountain
       </a>
       is highest, followed by
       <a href="/wiki/Pine_Log_Mountain" title="Pine Log Mountain">
        Pine Log Mountain
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sawnee_Mountain" title="Sawnee Mountain">
        Sawnee Mountain
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Hanging_Mountain&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Hanging Mountain (page does not exist)">
        Hanging Mountain
       </a>
       , followed by the others listed above. Stone, Sweat, Bear, and Sawnee are all home to some of
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Atlanta_broadcast_stations_by_location#Mountains" title="List of Atlanta broadcast stations by location">
        the area's broadcast stations
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The area's
       <a href="/wiki/Subsoil" title="Subsoil">
        subsoil
       </a>
       is a dense clay soil, colored
       <a href="/wiki/Rust" title="Rust">
        rusty
       </a>
       by the
       <a href="/wiki/Iron_oxide" title="Iron oxide">
        iron oxide
       </a>
       present in it. It becomes very
       <a href="/wiki/Mud" title="Mud">
        muddy
       </a>
       and sticky when wet, and hard when dry, and
       <a href="/wiki/Stain" title="Stain">
        stains
       </a>
       light-colored
       <a href="/wiki/Carpet" title="Carpet">
        carpets
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Clothing" title="Clothing">
        clothing
       </a>
       easily. It also tends to have a low
       <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">
        pH
       </a>
       , further aggravating gardeners. The fineness of it also means it is easily deposited into streams during heavy rains, creating
       <a href="/wiki/Silt" title="Silt">
        silt
       </a>
       problems where it is exposed due to construction. This transported red soil can be seen downstream on the
       <a href="/wiki/Bank_(geography)" title="Bank (geography)">
        riverbanks
       </a>
       of south Georgia (where the native clay is white), and down to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Florida_panhandle" title="Florida panhandle">
        Florida panhandle
       </a>
       (where the native
       <a href="/wiki/Sand" title="Sand">
        sand
       </a>
       is also white).
       <a href="/wiki/Topsoil" title="Topsoil">
        Topsoil
       </a>
       is present only in natural forest areas, created by the
       <a href="/wiki/Decomposition" title="Decomposition">
        decomposition
       </a>
       of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leaf_litter" title="Leaf litter">
        leaf litter
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Earthquakes_and_fault_lines">
        Earthquakes and fault lines
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlanta_metropolitan_area&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Earthquakes and fault lines">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       An extinct
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fault_line" title="Fault line">
        fault line
       </a>
       called the Brevard Fault runs roughly parallel to the
       <a href="/wiki/Chattahoochee_River" title="Chattahoochee River">
        Chattahoochee River
       </a>
       , but as its last movements were apparently
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prehistoric" title="Prehistoric">
        prehistoric
       </a>
       , it is considered extinct and not a threat to the region. Still, minor
       <a href="/wiki/Earthquake" title="Earthquake">
        earthquakes
       </a>
       do rattle the area (and all of Georgia) occasionally.
       <a href="/wiki/2003_Alabama_earthquake" title="2003 Alabama earthquake">
        One notable one was in April 2003
       </a>
       (magnitude 4.6) coming from the northwest, its
       <a href="/wiki/Epicenter" title="Epicenter">
        epicenter
       </a>
       just across the state line in northeastern Alabama. While many people slept through the 5
       <small>
        A.M.
       </small>
       quake, it caused a minor panic in others completely unaware of what was happening. Similar earthquakes occur in this region called the
       <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Tennessee_Seismic_Zone" title="Eastern Tennessee Seismic Zone">
        Eastern Tennessee Seismic Zone
       </a>
       , often felt much more widely across the stronger
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_crust" title="Earth's crust">
        crust
       </a>
       of eastern North America as compared to the west. Thus, the
       <a href="/wiki/1886_Charleston_earthquake" title="1886 Charleston earthquake">
        1886 Charleston, South Carolina earthquake
       </a>
       was also felt in Atlanta and throughout the Southeast. It caused damage as far as central Alabama and West Virginia. Two small earthquakes were also felt on the southeast side near
       <a href="/wiki/Eatonton,_Georgia" title="Eatonton, Georgia">
        Eatonton
       </a>
       in early April 2009. The
       <a href="/wiki/New_Madrid_Seismic_Zone" title="New Madrid Seismic Zone">
        New Madrid Seismic Zone
       </a>
       (near the Missouri-Tennessee borders) and the seismic zone producing the 1886 magnitude 7.3 earthquake are still capable of producing moderate or major earthquakes, in which the entire Atlanta area will feel moderately or even strongly.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">
        Climate
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlanta_metropolitan_area&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Climate">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Climate_of_Atlanta" title="Climate of Atlanta">
        Climate of Atlanta
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Atlanta metro area has a
       <a href="/wiki/Humid_subtropical_climate" title="Humid subtropical climate">
        humid subtropical climate
       </a>
       with four
       <a href="/wiki/Season" title="Season">
        seasons
       </a>
       , although summer is the longest. January daily lows average from 32–35 °F (0–2 °C) north to south, and highs range from 48–54 °F (9–12 °C), but often reach well above or below this average. There is an average annual snowfall of about 2.5 inches (6.4 cm), falling mostly from December through March, though there was snow north of the city on April 3, 1987. Snow flurries are actually common during the winter months when there is an especially deep trough in the jet stream. These events usually do not amount to more than a slight dusting and therefore go unrecognized in most weather summaries. Summers, by contrast, are long and consistently hot and humid, with July mornings averaging 71 °F (22 °C) and afternoons averaging 89 °F (32 °C), slight breezes, and typically a 20–40% chance of afternoon
       <a href="/wiki/Thunderstorm" title="Thunderstorm">
        thunderstorms
       </a>
       . During the summer afternoon thunderstorms, temperatures may suddenly drop to 70-77 degrees with locally heavy rainfall. Average annual rainfall is about 50.2 inches (1,280 mm), with late winter and early spring (as well as July) being the wettest and fall (especially October) being the driest.
      </p>
      <p>
       From 1878 to 2011, the highest recorded temperatures at Atlanta were 105 °F (40.6 °C) on three days in the extraordinarily hot July 1980, followed by 104 °F (40 °C) that month and in August 2007, the hottest month ever for the area. This was broken on the last day of June 2012, when the temperature reached 106 °F (41.1 °C), during a massive
       <a href="/wiki/Heat_wave" title="Heat wave">
        heat wave
       </a>
       that hit most of the country, with another 105 the next day tying the July record. The lowest recorded temperatures were −6 °F (−21 °C) and −8 °F (−22 °C) on January 20 and 21 of 1985, and −9 °F (−23 °C) on February 13, 1899, during severe cold snaps that went so far south they devastated the entire
       <a href="/wiki/Citrus" title="Citrus">
        citrus
       </a>
       industry in
       <a href="/wiki/Central_Florida" title="Central Florida">
        central Florida
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Opal" title="Hurricane Opal">
        Hurricane Opal
       </a>
       brought sustained
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tropical_storm" title="Tropical storm">
        tropical storm
       </a>
       conditions to the area one night in early October 1995, uprooting hundreds of
       <a href="/wiki/Tree" title="Tree">
        trees
       </a>
       and causing widespread
       <a href="/wiki/Power_outage" title="Power outage">
        power outages
       </a>
       , after soaking the area with rain for two days prior. Since 1950 some metro counties have been hit more than 20 times by tornadoes, with Cobb (26) and Fulton (22) being two of the highest in the state. The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dunwoody_tornado" title="Dunwoody tornado">
        Dunwoody tornado
       </a>
       in early April 1998 was the worst
       <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">
        tornado
       </a>
       to have struck the area. A
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2008_Atlanta_tornado" title="2008 Atlanta tornado">
        tornado struck downtown Atlanta
       </a>
       in March 2008, causing a half-billion dollars in damage, one of the most expensive storms ever recorded anywhere.
      </p>
      <p>
       The area experiences a
       <a href="/wiki/Winter_storm" title="Winter storm">
        winter storm
       </a>
       with significant
       <a href="/wiki/Snow" title="Snow">
        snowfall
       </a>
       about once each year, however this can be extremely irregular. A
       <a href="/wiki/Blizzard" title="Blizzard">
        blizzard
       </a>
       (see:
       <a href="/wiki/1993_Storm_of_the_Century" title="1993 Storm of the Century">
        1993 Storm of the Century
       </a>
       ) caught much of the Southeast off-guard in 1993, dumping 4.5 inches (11.4 cm) at the Atlanta airport on March 13, and much more than that in the suburbs to the north and west, as well as in the mountains. The only other recorded winter storm of comparable severity was the
       <a href="/wiki/Great_Blizzard_of_1899" title="Great Blizzard of 1899">
        Great Blizzard of 1899
       </a>
       . The heaviest snow, however, was in January 1940, when 8.3 inches (21.1 cm) buried the city during its coldest month on record. The second-heaviest was in 1983, when a very late storm dumped 7.9 inches (20.1 cm) on March 24.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ice_storms" title="Ice storms">
        Ice storms
       </a>
       have also occurred in the area. The well-remembered 1973 ice storm was brutal as was the storm in 1982.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Southeastern U.S. drought of 2006–2008 began with dry weather in 2006, and left area
       <a href="/wiki/Lake" title="Lake">
        lakes
       </a>
       very low. The drought finally began to abate significantly after the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2009_Atlanta_floods" title="2009 Atlanta floods">
        2009 Atlanta floods
       </a>
       , when some areas got up to 20 inches (500 mm) of rain in a week, with half of that falling in just 24 hours near the end of the period. The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USGS" title="USGS">
        USGS
       </a>
       calculated it to be a greater-than-
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/500-year_flood" title="500-year flood">
        500-year flood
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Environment">
        Environment
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlanta_metropolitan_area&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Environment">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The area's prolific rains are drained by many different
       <a href="/wiki/Stream" title="Stream">
        streams
       </a>
       and creeks. The main
       <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">
        basin
       </a>
       is that of the
       <a href="/wiki/Chattahoochee_River" title="Chattahoochee River">
        Chattahoochee River
       </a>
       , running northeast to southwest. The further northwestern suburbs drain into the
       <a href="/wiki/Etowah_River" title="Etowah River">
        Etowah River
       </a>
       via the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Little_River_(northern_Georgia)" title="Little River (northern Georgia)">
        Little River
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Lake_Allatoona" title="Lake Allatoona">
        Lake Allatoona
       </a>
       . The southern suburbs are drained by the
       <a href="/wiki/Flint_River_(Georgia)" title="Flint River (Georgia)">
        Flint River
       </a>
       , and the east-southeastern ones by the
       <a href="/wiki/Oconee_River" title="Oconee River">
        Oconee River
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Yellow_River_(Georgia)" title="Yellow River (Georgia)">
        Yellow River
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       By 2005 the metro area was using 360 million US gallons (1,400,000 m
       <sup>
        3
       </sup>
       ) of water per day (about 80 US gallons (300 L) per person per day) from these rivers. This usage was reduced by more than 10% during the drought, but soared back up after watering restrictions were eased (and before the flooding ensued). The need for water is seen as a barrier to further growth in the area, but permanent measures for non-emergency
       <a href="/wiki/Water_conservation" title="Water conservation">
        water conservation
       </a>
       have never been put in place.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       The state legislature has refused to pass a requirement for
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low-flow_toilet" title="Low-flow toilet">
        low-flow toilets
       </a>
       to be installed in homes that are sold, bowing to
       <a href="/wiki/Lobbying" title="Lobbying">
        pressure
       </a>
       from the
       <a href="/wiki/Real_estate" title="Real estate">
        real estate
       </a>
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Home_selling&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Home selling (page does not exist)">
        sales
       </a>
       industry.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Disputes over water are becoming increasingly common, with both
       <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">
        Alabama
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">
        Florida
       </a>
       filing
       <a href="/wiki/Lawsuit" title="Lawsuit">
        lawsuits
       </a>
       and threatening
       <a href="/wiki/Injunction" title="Injunction">
        injunctions
       </a>
       to prevent Georgia from taking too much water, mostly for metro Atlanta.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">
        South Carolina
       </a>
       also threatened when a
       <a href="/wiki/Pipeline_transport" title="Pipeline transport">
        pipeline
       </a>
       east to the
       <a href="/wiki/Savannah_River" title="Savannah River">
        Savannah River
       </a>
       was mentioned even informally.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       The state has now been ordered by a judge to reduce withdrawals from the Chattahoochee south of Lanier to 1970s levels within three years (2012), something that would create an immediate emergency water shortage if it were actually enforced.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       This was done because it was ruled the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Congress" title="U.S. Congress">
        U.S. Congress
       </a>
       never authorized the use of the lake as a
       <a href="/wiki/Water_supply" title="Water supply">
        water supply
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2016)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Flora">
        Flora
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlanta_metropolitan_area&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Flora">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The native
       <a href="/wiki/Forest" title="Forest">
        forest
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canopy_(forest)" title="Canopy (forest)">
        canopy
       </a>
       is mainly
       <a href="/wiki/Oak" title="Oak">
        oak
       </a>
       , redbud,
       <a href="/wiki/Hickory" title="Hickory">
        hickory
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Populus" title="Populus">
        poplar
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tuliptree" title="Tuliptree">
        tuliptree
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Pine" title="Pine">
        pine
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Liquidambar_styraciflua" title="Liquidambar styraciflua">
        sweetgum
       </a>
       , with
       <a href="/wiki/American_chestnut" title="American chestnut">
        chestnut
       </a>
       having been common decades before in what is now considered
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oak-hickory_forest" title="Oak-hickory forest">
        oak-hickory forest
       </a>
       . Traveling from the south, the metro area is generally the first area in which
       <a href="/wiki/Autumn_leaf_color" title="Autumn leaf color">
        autumn leaf color
       </a>
       can be seen, due to the different trees growing at the higher elevation and
       <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">
        latitude
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Understory" title="Understory">
        Underneath
       </a>
       , the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flowering_dogwood" title="Flowering dogwood">
        flowering dogwood
       </a>
       is very common, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_cherry" title="Black cherry">
        black cherry
       </a>
       are quite prolific, with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mulberry" title="Mulberry">
        mulberry
       </a>
       popping up sometimes as well.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sourwood" title="Sourwood">
        Sourwood
       </a>
       is also in its native range, and is easily identified by the fact that it turns fiery red in early October, much brighter and weeks earlier than most other trees (which usually peak in early November).
      </p>
      <p>
       Shrubby plants include
       <a href="/wiki/Blackberry" title="Blackberry">
        blackberry
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horsechestnut" title="Horsechestnut">
        horsechestnut
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sumac" title="Sumac">
        sumac
       </a>
       , and sometimes
       <a href="/wiki/Crataegus" title="Crataegus">
        hawthorn
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Parthenocissus_quinquefolia" title="Parthenocissus quinquefolia">
        Virginia creeper
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poison-ivy_(plant)" title="Poison-ivy (plant)">
        poison ivy
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Smilax" title="Smilax">
        briar
       </a>
       are common
       <a href="/wiki/Vine" title="Vine">
        vines
       </a>
       . The
       <a href="/wiki/Helianthus_porteri" title="Helianthus porteri">
        Confederate yellow daisy
       </a>
       is a
       <a href="/wiki/Wildflower" title="Wildflower">
        wildflower
       </a>
       native only to the area around Stone Mountain.
      </p>
      <p>
       Common garden plants include dogwood,
       <a href="/wiki/Azalea" title="Azalea">
        azalea
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Hydrangea" title="Hydrangea">
        hydrangea
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flowering_cherry" title="Flowering cherry">
        flowering cherry
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Maple" title="Maple">
        maples
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pin_oak" title="Pin oak">
        pin oak
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red-tip_photinia" title="Red-tip photinia">
        red-tip photinia
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Holly" title="Holly">
        holly
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Juniper" title="Juniper">
        juniper
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_white_pine" title="Eastern white pine">
        white pine
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Magnolia" title="Magnolia">
        magnolia
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bradford_pear" title="Bradford pear">
        Bradford pear
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Forsythia" title="Forsythia">
        forsythia
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Liriope_(plant)" title="Liriope (plant)">
        liriope
       </a>
       (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mondograss" title="Mondograss">
        mondograss
       </a>
       ), and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_ivy" title="English ivy">
        English ivy
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Lawn" title="Lawn">
        Lawns
       </a>
       can be either cool-season
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grass" title="Grass">
        grasses
       </a>
       like
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fescue" title="Fescue">
        fescue
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Rye" title="Rye">
        rye
       </a>
       , or warm-season like
       <a href="/wiki/Zoysia" title="Zoysia">
        zoysia
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bermudagrass" title="Bermudagrass">
        bermudagrass
       </a>
       which turn brown in late fall. A few
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homeowners_association" title="Homeowners association">
        homeowners associations
       </a>
       actually prohibit green grass in the winter.
      </p>
      <p>
       Native to the nearby mountains, maples are now one of the most common landscape trees for new homes and parking lots, giving their color in the fall instead of spring. When planted close to buildings (which provide shelter and radiate heat), they can retain some of their color into December, especially if November has been warm.
      </p>
      <p>
       Common
       <a href="/wiki/Lawn" title="Lawn">
        lawn
       </a>
       weeds are
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Potentilla_indica" title="Potentilla indica">
        mock strawberry
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Violet_(plant)" title="Violet (plant)">
        violet
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Allium" title="Allium">
        wild onion
       </a>
       , and of course the ubiquitous
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dandelion" title="Dandelion">
        dandelion
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crabgrass" title="Crabgrass">
        crabgrass
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Plantago" title="Plantago">
        plantain
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       By far the most notorious
       <a href="/wiki/Introduced_species" title="Introduced species">
        introduced species
       </a>
       is
       <a href="/wiki/Kudzu" title="Kudzu">
        kudzu
       </a>
       , a highly
       <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">
        invasive species
       </a>
       from
       <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">
        Japan
       </a>
       which climbs and smothers trees and shrubs. New effective herbicides as well as increased development of formerly rural areas has greatly reduced kudzu in the metro area (although still quite common elsewhere in Georgia). Wisteria planted decades ago by farmers in then-rural areas has become wild and is common in undeveloped forests. Some vines exceed 50 years of age and cover dozens of acres of forest, creating a dense, purple explosion each spring. Japanese honeysuckle is extremely common; its fragrance an early summer delight. A common garden plant, the Chinese privet, has escaped to become the state's most invasive non-native plant species.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Fauna">
        Fauna
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlanta_metropolitan_area&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Fauna">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Among
       <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">
        mammals
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Eastern_gray_squirrel" title="Eastern gray squirrel">
        eastern gray squirrel
       </a>
       is by far the most ubiquitous, stealing
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birdseed" title="Birdseed">
        birdseed
       </a>
       from the
       <a href="/wiki/Bird_feeder" title="Bird feeder">
        bird feeders
       </a>
       which many locals maintain.
       <a href="/wiki/Chipmunk" title="Chipmunk">
        Chipmunks
       </a>
       and small brown
       <a href="/wiki/Rabbit" title="Rabbit">
        rabbits
       </a>
       are common, but it is relatively rare to hear of them doing any damage.
       <a href="/wiki/Opossum" title="Opossum">
        Opossum
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Raccoon" title="Raccoon">
        raccoons
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Fox" title="Fox">
        foxes
       </a>
       , and now even small
       <a href="/wiki/Coyote" title="Coyote">
        coyotes
       </a>
       and armadillos are frequently seen. Garden and meadow snakes are common; three venomous snakes (
       <a href="/wiki/Eastern_diamondback_rattlesnake" title="Eastern diamondback rattlesnake">
        Eastern diamondback rattlesnake
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Agkistrodon_piscivorus" title="Agkistrodon piscivorus">
        Water Moccasin
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Agkistrodon_contortrix" title="Agkistrodon contortrix">
        Copperhead
       </a>
       ) are indigenous, but reports of bites are rare. Many types of frogs, including tree frogs and bullfrogs, are easily heard in early summer, as are
       <a href="/wiki/Cicada" title="Cicada">
        cicadas
       </a>
       in July and August.
       <a href="/wiki/American_black_bear" title="American black bear">
        Black bears
       </a>
       occasionally wander down from the mountains, and
       <a href="/wiki/White-tailed_deer" title="White-tailed deer">
        white-tailed deer
       </a>
       are abundant; overpopulated in some areas. Homeowners in the outer suburbs are prone to landscaping damage due to scavenging deer.
      </p>
      <p>
       The most common
       <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">
        birds
       </a>
       are the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brown_Thrasher" title="Brown Thrasher">
        Brown Thrasher
       </a>
       (the GA
       <a href="/wiki/State_bird" title="State bird">
        state bird
       </a>
       ),
       <a href="/wiki/American_crow" title="American crow">
        American crow
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Common_starling" title="Common starling">
        European (or common) starling
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/American_robin" title="American robin">
        American robin
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Mourning_dove" title="Mourning dove">
        mourning dove
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/House_sparrow" title="House sparrow">
        house sparrow
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Northern_cardinal" title="Northern cardinal">
        northern cardinal
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Purple_finch" title="Purple finch">
        purple finch
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Carolina_chickadee" title="Carolina chickadee">
        Carolina chickadee
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tufted_titmouse" title="Tufted titmouse">
        tufted titmouse
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bluejay" title="Bluejay">
        bluejay
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/White-breasted_nuthatch" title="White-breasted nuthatch">
        white-breasted nuthatch
       </a>
       , eastern bluebird, mockingbird,
       <a href="/wiki/Brown-headed_nuthatch" title="Brown-headed nuthatch">
        brown-headed nuthatch
       </a>
       , and the
       <a href="/wiki/Carolina_wren" title="Carolina wren">
        Carolina wren
       </a>
       . Birds of prey thrive in the area, with three varieties of hawks common near open fields in even the most populated areas. Falcons roost on skyscrapers in downtown Atlanta and can be regularly seen feasting on pigeons. The American kestrel is sometimes seen. Late in the year, three species of owls can be heard nightly in wooded areas. Various
       <a href="/wiki/Woodpecker" title="Woodpecker">
        woodpeckers
       </a>
       can be seen in forested lots, including the
       <a href="/wiki/Red-bellied_woodpecker" title="Red-bellied woodpecker">
        red-bellied woodpecker
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Northern_flicker" title="Northern flicker">
        northern flicker
       </a>
       (also known as the "red-shafted flicker"), and the
       <a href="/wiki/Downy_woodpecker" title="Downy woodpecker">
        downy woodpecker
       </a>
       . The red-headed woodpecker is common in open fields and on golf courses. The
       <a href="/wiki/American_goldfinch" title="American goldfinch">
        American goldfinch
       </a>
       is present mostly in winter, and the
       <a href="/wiki/Ruby-throated_hummingbird" title="Ruby-throated hummingbird">
        ruby-throated hummingbird
       </a>
       only in summer.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Government_and_politics">
        Government and politics
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlanta_metropolitan_area&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Government and politics">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MariettaGeorgia.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1524" data-file-width="2032" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/MariettaGeorgia.jpg/200px-MariettaGeorgia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/MariettaGeorgia.jpg/300px-MariettaGeorgia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/MariettaGeorgia.jpg/400px-MariettaGeorgia.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MariettaGeorgia.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Downtown Marietta's historic
         <a href="/wiki/Town_square" title="Town square">
          town square
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Georgia has the smallest average county size of any state which operates county governments.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2008)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       This focuses government more locally but allows greater conflict between multiple jurisdictions, each with its own agenda.
      </p>
      <p>
       The first significant
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Intergovernmental_agency&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Intergovernmental agency (page does not exist)">
        intergovernmental agency
       </a>
       in metro Atlanta was the
       <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Atlanta_Rapid_Transit_Authority" title="Metropolitan Atlanta Rapid Transit Authority">
        Metropolitan Atlanta Rapid Transit Authority
       </a>
       , which runs the MARTA
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_transportation" title="Public transportation">
        public transportation
       </a>
       system. Alongside other factors such as
       <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">
        race
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Socioeconomic_class" title="Socioeconomic class">
        class
       </a>
       , as well as a lack of planning and perceived lack of need, problems associated with the
       <a href="/wiki/Inner_city" title="Inner city">
        inner city
       </a>
       of Atlanta (
       <a href="/wiki/Crime" title="Crime">
        crime
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">
        poverty
       </a>
       , and poor
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_school_(government_funded)" title="Public school (government funded)">
        public school
       </a>
       performance) influenced Cobb, Gwinnett, and Clayton county voters to refuse to allow construction of MARTA into their respective counties during the 1970s. These decisions resulted in permanent effects on
       <a href="/wiki/Land_development" title="Land development">
        land development
       </a>
       in the region, making use of private
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Automobile" title="Automobile">
        automobiles
       </a>
       even more of a necessity.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Atlanta_Regional_Commission" title="Atlanta Regional Commission">
        Atlanta Regional Commission
       </a>
       is so far the closest that the area has come to a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metropolitan_government" title="Metropolitan government">
        metropolitan government
       </a>
       . It approves only those projects deemed to have a positive effect beyond the immediate area in which they are to be constructed. The
       <a href="/wiki/Georgia_Regional_Transportation_Authority" title="Georgia Regional Transportation Authority">
        Georgia Regional Transportation Authority
       </a>
       is somewhat of a cross between ARC and MARTA, working to improve mobility, air quality, and land use practices in the region. GRTA also operates
       <a href="/wiki/Georgia_Regional_Transportation_Authority#''''Xpress''''_service" title="Georgia Regional Transportation Authority">
        Xpress buses
       </a>
       from 11 counties, and could operate
       <a href="/wiki/Commuter_rail" title="Commuter rail">
        commuter rail
       </a>
       service in the future. Currently, plans for commuter rail and eventual
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intercity_rail" title="Intercity rail">
        intercity rail
       </a>
       (including the long-proposed but still unfunded
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlanta_Multimodal_Passenger_Terminal" title="Atlanta Multimodal Passenger Terminal">
        Atlanta Multimodal Passenger Terminal
       </a>
       ) are the responsibility of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Georgia_Rail_Passenger_Authority" title="Georgia Rail Passenger Authority">
        Georgia Rail Passenger Authority
       </a>
       , which receives almost no funding.
      </p>
      <p>
       Since 2007 proposals have been floated to allow new multi-county
       <a href="/wiki/Sales_tax" title="Sales tax">
        sales taxes
       </a>
       , in addition to existing county sales taxes for roads, to pay for regional transportation initiatives.
       <a class="external autonumber" href="http://www.ajc.com/metro/content/metro/legis07/stories/2007/01/05/transportation.html" rel="nofollow">
        [2]
       </a>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">
        Demographics
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Atlanta_metropolitan_area&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Demographics">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Atlanta#Metro_Atlanta" title="Demographics of Atlanta">
        Demographics of Metro Atlanta
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The 2010 census counted 5,268,860 people in the 28-county metro area. This was an increase of 1,020,879 versus the same 28-county area in 2000, second only to
       <a href="/wiki/Houston" title="Houston">
        Houston
       </a>
       . The percent increase was 24.0%, second-highest (after Houston) among the nation's ten largest metro areas.
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
         Race, ethnicity, or
         <br/>
         foreign-born status
        </th>
        <th>
         Pop. 2010
        </th>
        <th>
         % of total 2010
        </th>
        <th>
         Pop. 2000
         <sup class="reference" id="ref_scopeA">
          <a href="#endnote_scopeA">
           [A]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </th>
        <th>
         % of total 2000
        </th>
        <th>
         absolute
         <br/>
         change 2000-2010
         <sup class="reference" id="ref_relationB">
          <a href="#endnote_relationB">
           [B]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </th>
        <th>
         % change 2000-2010
         <sup class="reference" id="ref_relationB">
          <a href="#endnote_relationB">
           [B]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <b>
          Total
         </b>
        </td>
